<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="An atlas of over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences provides insight into crucifer regulatory regions"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2013 45:8"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-06-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mathieu Blanchette and colleagues report whole-genome sequencing of three Brassicaceae species, Leavenworthia alabamica, Sisymbrium irio and Aethionema arabicum. They include comparative genomic analysis with 6 additional crucifier genomes, identify and characterize over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences and provide a map of functional noncoding regions in plant genomes. Despite the central importance of noncoding DNA to gene regulation and evolution, understanding of the extent of selection on plant noncoding DNA remains limited compared to that of other organisms. Here we report sequencing of genomes from three Brassicaceae species (Leavenworthia alabamica, Sisymbrium irio and Aethionema arabicum) and their joint analysis with six previously sequenced crucifer genomes. Conservation across orthologous bases suggests that at least 17% of the Arabidopsis thaliana genome is under selection, with nearly one-quarter of the sequence under selection lying outside of coding regions. Much of this sequence can be localized to approximately 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) that show evidence of transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulation. Population genomics analyses of two crucifer species, A. thaliana and Capsella grandiflora, confirm that most of the identified CNSs are evolving under medium to strong purifying selection. Overall, these CNSs highlight both similarities and several key differences between the regulatory DNA of plants and other species."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Mathieu Blanchette and colleagues report whole-genome sequencing of three Brassicaceae species, Leavenworthia alabamica, Sisymbrium irio and Aethionema arabicum. They include comparative genomic analysis with 6 additional crucifier genomes, identify and characterize over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences and provide a map of functional noncoding regions in plant genomes."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Despite the central importance of noncoding DNA to gene regulation and evolution, understanding of the extent of selection on plant noncoding DNA remains limited compared to that of other organisms. Here we report sequencing of genomes from three Brassicaceae species (<i>Leavenworthia alabamica</i>, <i>Sisymbrium irio</i> and <i>Aethionema arabicum</i>) and their joint analysis with six previously sequenced crucifer genomes. Conservation across orthologous bases suggests that at least 17% of the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome is under selection, with nearly one-quarter of the sequence under selection lying outside of coding regions. Much of this sequence can be localized to approximately 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) that show evidence of transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulation. Population genomics analyses of two crucifer species, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>Capsella grandiflora</i>, confirm that most of the identified CNSs are evolving under medium to strong purifying selection. Overall, these CNSs highlight both similarities and several key differences between the regulatory DNA of plants and other species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>A central challenge in functional and evolutionary genomics has been to determine the parts of a genome that are under selective constraint. Whereas protein-coding regions are relatively straightforward to identify, other functional elements such as transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulatory regions may be short and lacking in clear sequence signatures that would allow them to be detected in a single genome. Comparative genomic analyses across a group of closely related species provide a powerful approach to identify functional noncoding regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Duret, L. &amp; Bucher, P. Searching for regulatory elements in human noncoding sequences. Curr. Opin. Struct. Biol. 7, 399–406 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d22561e998">1</a></sup>. Over evolutionary time, non-functional sequences are expected to diverge faster than sequences under selective constraint. Patterns of sequence conservation may therefore be used to detect the footprints of functional noncoding elements. It is now widely accepted that the most powerful approach to phylogenetic footprinting is one based on a large number of species that have substantial aggregate divergence yet remain sufficiently closely related that the loss or displacement of functional elements is rare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Boffelli, D. et al. Phylogenetic shadowing of primate sequences to find functional regions of the human genome. Science 299, 1391–1394 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1002">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Hong, R.L., Hamaguchi, L., Busch, M.A. &amp; Weigel, D. Regulatory elements of the floral homeotic gene AGAMOUS identified by phylogenetic footprinting and shadowing. Plant Cell 15, 1296–1309 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1005">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Comparative genomic studies have led to the identification of thousands of CNSs in, among others, vertebrates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bejerano, G. et al. Ultraconserved elements in the human genome. Science 304, 1321–1325 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1012">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Siepel, A. et al. Evolutionarily conserved elements in vertebrate, insect, worm, and yeast genomes. Genome Res. 15, 1034–1050 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1015">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Margulies, E.H. et al. Analyses of deep mammalian sequence alignments and constraint predictions for 1% of the human genome. Genome Res. 17, 760–774 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1018">6</a></sup>, fruit flies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Stark, A. et al. Discovery of functional elements in 12 Drosophila genomes using evolutionary signatures. Nature 450, 219–232 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1022">7</a></sup> and yeast<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kellis, M., Patterson, N., Endrizzi, M., Birren, B. &amp; Lander, E.S. Sequencing and comparison of yeast species to identify genes and regulatory elements. Nature 423, 241–254 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1026">8</a></sup>. These CNSs are thought to be involved in diverse regulatory functions, including transcription initiation and transcript processing (for example, splicing or mRNA localization), as well as being implicated in complex patterning, such as embryonic development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Adrian, J. et al. cis-Regulatory elements and chromatin state coordinately control temporal and spatial expression of FLOWERING LOCUS T in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 22, 1425–1440 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1030">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1033">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Subramaniam, S. Conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) in higher plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 12, 126–132 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1036">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zou, C. et al. Cis-regulatory code of stress-responsive transcription in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 14992–14997 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1039">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hsieh, T.F. et al. Regulation of imprinted gene expression in Arabidopsis endosperm. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1755–1762 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1042">13</a></sup>. Plant CNSs have previously been identified on a genome-wide scale on the basis of the comparison of few or distant genomes (for example, maize versus rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Inada, D.C. et al. Conserved noncoding sequences in the grasses. Genome Res. 13, 2030–2041 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1046">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guo, H. &amp; Moose, S.P. Conserved noncoding sequences among cultivated cereal genomes identify candidate regulatory sequence elements and patterns of promoter evolution. Plant Cell 15, 1143–1158 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1049">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kaplinsky, N.J., Braun, D.M., Penterman, J., Goff, S.A. &amp; Freeling, M. Utility and distribution of conserved noncoding sequences in the grasses. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 6147–6151 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1052">16</a></sup>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> versus rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Bossolini, E., Wicker, T., Knobel, P.A. &amp; Keller, B. Comparison of orthologous loci from small grass genomes Brachypodium and rice: implications for wheat genomics and grass genome annotation. Plant J. 49, 704–717 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1060">17</a></sup>, <i>A. thaliana</i> versus <i>Brassica oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Colinas, J., Birnbaum, K. &amp; Benfey, P.N. Using cauliflower to find conserved non-coding regions in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 129, 451–454 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1069">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Haberer, G. et al. Large-scale cis-element detection by analysis of correlated expression and sequence conservation between Arabidopsis and Brassica oleracea. Plant Physiol. 142, 1589–1602 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1072">19</a></sup> and sets of diverse angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hupalo, D. &amp; Kern, A.D. Conservation and functional element discovery in 20 angiosperm plant genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. published online; 10.1093/molbev/mst082 (27 May 2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1076">20</a></sup>). This approach limits either the specificity provided by large divergence times or the sensitivity provided by the comparison of more closely related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1081">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Subramaniam, S. Conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) in higher plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 12, 126–132 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1084">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Reineke, A.R., Bornberg-Bauer, E. &amp; Gu, J. Evolutionary divergence and limits of conserved non-coding sequence detection in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, 6029–6043 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1087">21</a></sup>. Comparisons of paralogous noncoding regions flanking duplicated genes have also provided key insights into functional noncoding elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kaplinsky, N.J., Braun, D.M., Penterman, J., Goff, S.A. &amp; Freeling, M. Utility and distribution of conserved noncoding sequences in the grasses. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 99, 6147–6151 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1091">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Thomas, B.C., Rapaka, L., Lyons, E., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Arabidopsis intragenomic conserved noncoding sequence. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 3348–3353 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1094">22</a></sup>, but intraspecies duplicated CNSs may often experience relaxed selective constraints.</p><p>The Brassicaceae are an ideal family for the identification of CNSs owing to their relatively small genome sizes, robust phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1101">23</a></sup> and wealth of genomic data. So far, the genomes of six crucifer species have been partially or completely sequenced, including those of (i) the model plant <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1107">24</a></sup>; (ii) <i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i>, a congener of <i>A. thaliana</i> with a more ancestral karyotype and genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1117">25</a></sup>; (iii) <i>Capsella rubella</i>, which falls in the sister group to the genus <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Slotte, T. et al. The Capsella rubella genome and the genomic consequences of rapid mating system evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 831–835 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1127">26</a></sup>; (iv) <i>Brassica rapa</i> (Chinese cabbage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1134">27</a></sup>), one of the several closely related <i>Brassica</i> crop species in the tribe Brassiceae that share a recent genome triplication event (Br-α)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell published online; 10.1105/tpc.113.110486 (7 May 2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1142">28</a></sup>; (v) <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i> (previously <i>Thellungiella halophila</i>) of the tribe Eutremeae, an extremophile adapted to saline habitats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, R. et al. The reference genome of the halophytic plant Eutrema salsugineum. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 46 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1152">29</a></sup>; and (vi) <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i> (previously <i>Thellungiella parvula</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913–918 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1163">30</a></sup>, another extremophile of uncertain tribal placement.</p><p>To complement the set of previously published Brassicaceae genomes, we have sequenced the genomes of three additional species selected, on the basis of previously published phylogenetic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schranz, M.E., Song, B.H., Windsor, A.J. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: a family-wide perspective. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 10, 168–175 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1170">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Couvreur, T.L. et al. Molecular phylogenetics, temporal diversification, and principles of evolution in the mustard family (Brassicaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 55–71 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1173">32</a></sup>, to provide a broad diversity of lineages within the family. We took advantage of these nine closely related genome sequences to identify and characterize over 90,000 CNSs. The extent of selection acting on them was determined using a combination of comparative and population genomics data. Several lines of computational and experimental evidence point to a large proportion of CNSs having a role in transcriptional or post-transcriptional regulation. A full catalog of CNSs and their associated annotations in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> are available via a genome browser.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>To supplement six publicly available crucifer genomes, we sequenced and partially assembled the genomes of three further crucifers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Tab1">Table 1</a> and Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a>): (i) <i>Leavenworthia alabamica</i> (lineage 1 in the tribe Camelineae), a model plant species with recently lost self-incompatibility in some populations; (ii) <i>Sisymbrium irio</i> (lineage 2 in the tribe Sisymbrieae), a self-compatible annual closely related to the <i>Brassica</i> genus but lacking the derived whole-genome triplication; and (iii) <i>Aethionema arabicum</i> (tribe Aethionemeae), a self-compatible, early branching sister group to the remainder of the core Brassicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Couvreur, T.L. et al. Molecular phylogenetics, temporal diversification, and principles of evolution in the mustard family (Brassicaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 27, 55–71 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1214">32</a></sup>. All species share the ancient whole-genome duplication that occurred at the base of the family (At-α; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Bowers, J.E., Chapman, B.A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433–438 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1217">33</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics and gene content</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2684/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Assemblies of these three genomes included orthologs for the majority of <i>A. thaliana</i> protein-coding genes (68–83%, on a par with those found in more completely assembled genomes) and 98–99% of the ultraconserved core eukaryotic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K., Ning, Z., Keane, T. &amp; Korf, I. Assessing the gene space in draft genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 289–297 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1870">34</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Tab1">Table 1</a>), suggesting that the coverage of non-repetitive DNA was high. Furthermore, the scaffold size (N50 of 70–135 kb) was suitable for the identification of orthologous regions through synteny.</p><p>Protein-coding genes and transposable elements (TEs) were predicted across all nine genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Tab1">Table 1</a>, Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The number of annotated genes varied between species from 23,167 genes in <i>A. arabicum</i> to 41,174 in <i>B. rapa</i>. This variation was expected given the rediploidization process following the At-α duplication and several whole-genome amplifications after At-α (for example, the <i>Brassica</i> triplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell published online; 10.1105/tpc.113.110486 (7 May 2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1899">28</a></sup>). TEs comprised the majority of the variation in genome size observed across the crucifers, varying in content from 13–15% in the smallest genomes (<i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>S. parvula</i>) to <span class="stix">∼</span>50% in <i>E. salsugineum</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Multiple-genome comparison</h3><p>Nine-way genome alignments were generated for the crucifer genomes, using either <i>A. lyrata</i> or <i>A. thaliana</i> as the reference (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a>). A region of a non-reference genome was only allowed to align to a single region of the reference genome, although many regions from non-reference genomes were allowed to map to the same reference genome region (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). This ensured that lone paralogous genes resulting from the At-α duplication did not contaminate the alignments, while allowing more recent duplication events, such as the whole-genome triplication in <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1934">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lysak, M.A., Koch, M.A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516–525 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1937">35</a></sup>, to be represented. Local pairwise alignment blocks were filtered to only retain those that belonged to long sets of collinear blocks (chains). The vast majority of the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome belonged to a single such chain in each of the other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 2</a>), leaving little doubt about correct orthology. This pattern of alignment also provided strong support for the notion that most of the gene loss after At-α duplication was substantially completed before the divergence of species within the Brassicaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Schnable, J.C., Wang, X., Pires, J.C. &amp; Freeling, M. Escape from preferential retention following repeated whole genome duplications in plants. Front. Plant Sci. 3, 94 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1951">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Edger, P.P. &amp; Pires, J.C. Gene and genome duplications: the impact of dosage-sensitivity on the fate of nuclear genes. Chromosome Res. 17, 699–717 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d22561e1954">37</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome alignments and whole-genome triplications."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genome alignments and whole-genome triplications.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="329"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Typical example of a 160-kb <i>A. lyrata</i> region aligning against the other eight Brassicaceae genomes. Alignment blocks (solid rectangles) are linked to form collinear chains. In alignments with most species, most positions in <i>A. lyrata</i> are contained within a single alignment chain, except in alignments with <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>L. alabamica</i>, where three alignment chains each were seen, suggesting whole-genome triplications in these two species. (<b>b</b>) Genome-wide level of chain coverage by each of the eight species of the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome. (<b>c</b>–<b>f</b>) Comparative chromosome painting (CCP) analysis in <i>L. alabamica</i> (2<i>n</i> = 22 chromosomes). (<b>c</b>) DAPI-stained mitotic chromosomes. Chromosomes of different size and heterochromatin content suggest an allopolyploid origin of the species. Arrowheads mark the four largest chromosomes. (<b>d</b>–<b>f</b>) CCP analysis of Cardamineae-specific ancestral chromosomes on pachytene (meiotic) chromosome spreads. (<b>d</b>) Three genomic copies of the genomic block A (the A1 copy is split between two different chromosomes). (<b>e</b>) Three genomic copies of chromosome AK6/8. (<b>f</b>) Two genomic copies of chromosome AK8/6; the third copy is absent. In <b>e</b>,<b>f</b>, copy 1 being substantially longer than the other homeolog(s) suggests an allohexaploid origin or differential fractionation of the three subgenomes. All scale bars, 10 μm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>However, two species showed a strong departure from this essentially diploid organization. As expected, most <i>A. lyrata</i> regions were spanned by three long alignment chains in the <i>B. rapa</i> genome, owing to the established Br-α triplication event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig1">Fig. 1a,b</a>). Notably, the <i>L. alabamica</i> genome showed the same clear signs of an independent whole-genome triplication, referred to hereafter as La-α. Comparative chromosome painting of the <i>L. alabamica</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig1">Fig. 1c–f</a>) independently supported the establishment of hexaploidy after At-α, with the patterning of <i>A. thaliana</i> BAC probes supporting the retention of some chromosomal regions in two copies and others in three copies.</p><p>A phylogenetic tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) derived from 1,048,889 fourfold-degenerate sites and using <i>Carica papaya</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991–996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2071">38</a></sup> as an outgroup was consistent with previously published phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schranz, M.E., Song, B.H., Windsor, A.J. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: a family-wide perspective. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 10, 168–175 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2075">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bailey, C.D. et al. Toward a global phylogeny of the Brassicaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 2142–2160 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2078">39</a></sup>. The total branch length of the tree (<span class="stix">∼</span>1.5 substitutions per site) was similar to that for a set of nine diverse mammals (<span class="stix">∼</span>1.3 substitutions per site)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Thomas, J.W. et al. Comparative analyses of multi-species sequences from targeted genomic regions. Nature 424, 788–793 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2082">40</a></sup> used in the identification of conserved noncoding regions. The divergence between <i>L. alabamica</i> paralogs (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.3 substitutions per site) was similar to that between the paralogs of <i>B. rapa</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.35 substitutions per site), formed <span class="stix">∼</span>24 (18–28) million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yang, Y.W., Lai, K.N., Tai, P.Y. &amp; Li, W.H. Rates of nucleotide substitution in angiosperm mitochondrial DNA sequences and dates of divergence between Brassica and other angiosperm lineages. J. Mol. Evol. 48, 597–604 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2093">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Town, C.D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348–1359 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2096">42</a></sup> by the Br-α triplication. Although this slightly larger number of substitutions per site may not imply a more recent event, because of the possibility of variation in neutral substitution rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yang, L. &amp; Gaut, B.S. Factors that contribute to variation in evolutionary rate among Arabidopsis genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2359–2369 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2100">43</a></sup>, it likely indicates a similar era for these independent hexaploidization events.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="A phylogenetic tree obtained using a set of 1,048,889 fourfold-degenerate sites in PhyML (general time-reversible (GTR) substitution model)73."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: A phylogenetic tree obtained using a set of 1,048,889 fourfold-degenerate sites in PhyML (general time-reversible (GTR) substitution model)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Guindon, S., Lethiec, F., Duroux, P. &amp; Gascuel, O. PHYML Online—a web server for fast maximum likelihood–based phylogenetic inference. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W557–W559 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2114">73</a></sup>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="675" height="484"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The positions (triangles) of the At-α duplication event and of the two whole-genome triplication events (Br-α and La-α) are approximate.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Selection on noncoding sites in the crucifer genomes</h3><p>Comparisons of multiple closely related genomes allows the fraction of the genome that is constrained by selection to be estimated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Ponting, C.P. &amp; Hardison, R.C. What fraction of the human genome is functional? Genome Res. 21, 1769–1776 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2136">44</a></sup>. PhyloP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pollard, K.S., Hubisz, M.J., Rosenbloom, K.R. &amp; Siepel, A. Detection of nonneutral substitution rates on mammalian phylogenies. Genome Res. 20, 110–121 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2140">45</a></sup> was used to measure interspecies conservation of each nucleotide of the <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> genomes, independent of the flanking nucleotides. Because of the insufficient level of divergence between the nine species considered, these scores could not unambiguously distinguish individual constrained sites from neutral ones. However, the proportion of sites under selection in the whole genome or in any given subset of sites could be estimated by comparing the distribution of PhyloP scores across the genome to that at fourfold-degenerate sites, which are largely unconstrained<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Margulies, E.H. &amp; Birney, E. Approaches to comparative sequence analysis: towards a functional view of vertebrate genomes. Nat. Rev. Genet. 9, 303–313 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2150">46</a></sup> (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a>).</p><p>At least 17.7% of the assembled <i>A. thaliana</i> genome sequence (21.1 Mb) seemed to be evolving under constraint (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), with close to a quarter of this sequence (4.5 Mb) located outside of protein-coding regions. In the larger TE-rich <i>A. lyrata</i> genome, very slightly more sites seemed to be under selection (22.2 Mb), corresponding to a much smaller fraction of the genome (11.3%). Consequently, the major cause of the difference in genome size between <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> is probably not the loss of functional sites but rather the loss of effectively unconstrained regions in <i>A. thaliana</i>, likely coupled with higher recent TE activity in <i>A. lyrata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hu, T.T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nat. Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2182">25</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Estimation of the fraction of sites under selection in the A. thaliana genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Estimation of the fraction of sites under selection in the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="862"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Breakdown of sites under selection between coding (CDS) and noncoding regions (left) and among different types of noncoding regions (right). (<b>b</b>) Percentage of sites under selection for different sets of regions of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome. Noncoding region categories include UTRs: 5′ (blue) and 3′ (light blue); intronic regions: 500 bp of intron 1 (tan), 30-bp intronic regions flanking exons (orange) and middle of introns other than intron 1 (brown); and intergenic regions: bases are assigned to the closest annotated TSS or TES.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In both <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, the constrained noncoding sites were divided roughly evenly between transcript-associated sites (introns and UTRs) and intergenic sites (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). The proportion of sites under selection was particularly high in 5′ and 3′ UTRs (17% and 13%, respectively) as well as in intronic regions flanking exons (15% within 30 bp of splice sites) but was much lower in the center of introns (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Contrary to what is observed in mammals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Sorek, R. &amp; Ast, G. Intronic sequences flanking alternatively spliced exons are conserved between human and mouse. Genome Res. 13, 1631–1637 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2238">47</a></sup> and <i>Drosophila melanogaster</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Halligan, D.L., Eyre-Walker, A., Andolfatto, P. &amp; Keightley, P.D. Patterns of evolutionary constraints in intronic and intergenic DNA of Drosophila. Genome Res. 14, 273–279 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2244">48</a></sup>, intronic bases located within 500 bp of the transcription start site (TSS) did not seem to be under significantly stronger selective pressure than other intronic bases.</p><p>Outside protein-coding transcripts, the proportion of sites under selection decreased with the distance from the TSS. Nonetheless, as observed in <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Halligan, D.L. &amp; Keightley, P.D. Ubiquitous selective constraints in the Drosophila genome revealed by a genome-wide interspecies comparison. Genome Res. 16, 875–884 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2253">49</a></sup>, more than half of intergenic sites showing signs of selection were &gt;1 kb away from the closest annotated TSS. Notably, and in contrast to findings in mammals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Blanchette, M. et al. Genome-wide computational prediction of transcriptional regulatory modules reveals new insights into human gene expression. Genome Res. 16, 656–668 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2257">50</a></sup>, evidence of constraint was lowest immediately downstream of 3′ UTRs, suggesting that regulatory elements are rarely located in those regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">At least 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences</h3><p>The PhyloP analyses yielded estimates of the number of individual sites under selection in specific portions of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome, but they did not pinpoint the location of those sites. Constrained sites can only be reliably identified if they cluster with other such sites, forming CNSs. CNSs are genomic regions that show a reduced mutation rate over contiguous or near-contiguous sets of noncoding bases. A set of 92,421 (90,104) CNSs was identified in the <i>A. lyrata</i> (<i>A. thaliana</i>) genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>) using a pipeline based on PhastCons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Siepel, A. et al. Evolutionarily conserved elements in vertebrate, insect, worm, and yeast genomes. Genome Res. 15, 1034–1050 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2281">5</a></sup>. Evidence of selection was also clearly provided by the relative rarity of insertions and deletions within CNSs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Previously published CNSs obtained from pairwise genome comparisons were typically identified in this screen, but five- to tenfold more conserved regions were also identified, owing to the sensitivity afforded by the use of multiple genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>Each CNS was annotated in accordance with its position relative to genes in the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome. CNSs without evidence of expression in whole <i>A. thaliana</i> or <i>A. lyrata</i> plants (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a>) were classified as proximal upstream or downstream (&lt;500 bp upstream of the TSS or downstream of a TES, respectively), distal (&gt;500 bp away from any gene's TSS or TES) or ambiguous. Genic CNSs were subdivided on the basis of their location in 5′ UTRs, introns and 3′ UTRs. We also identified 820 CNSs with substantial evidence of short RNA expression in <i>A. lyrata</i> (Online <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec13">Methods</a>) and labeled these as putative small noncoding RNA CNSs (smRNA CNSs). Conserved regions with evidence of small RNA expression in other plants but not in <i>A. lyrata</i> were set apart as a class of potential smRNA CNSs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p>CNS density in different types of genomic regions closely followed that of the inferred sites under selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Crucifer CNSs were typically short (median length of 36 bp and slightly shorter in introns and UTRs) and had a GC content similar to that of the noncoding portion of the genome (25–40%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). smRNA CNSs, most of which corresponded with known noncoding RNA genes, formed a relatively distinct group, showing higher conservation and GC content and a markedly bimodal size distribution, mostly caused by large numbers of microRNA (miRNA) and tRNA genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evidence of purifying selection on CNSs at the population level</h3><p>To independently assess evidence for purifying selection acting on CNSs, we analyzed the distribution of sequence diversity in CNSs within the populations of two Brassicaceae species: a recently sequenced set of 80 <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Cao, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of multiple Arabidopsis thaliana populations. Nat. Genet. 43, 956–963 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2341">51</a></sup> and a set of 13 outbred individuals (26 haplotypes) of <i>C. grandiflora</i> (S.I.W., unpublished data), a close relative of <i>C. rubella</i>. Evidence for recent purifying selection acting on CNSs was found in the minor allele frequency (MAF) spectra of both populations. Both species showed an excess of rare variants in the CNS bases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>) and reduced levels of population diversity compared to fourfold-degenerate sites, as measured by nucleotide diversity <i>π</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nei, M. &amp; Li, W.H. Mathematical model for studying genetic variation in terms of restriction endonucleases. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 76, 5269–5273 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2357">52</a>) and Watterson's estimator <i>θ</i><sub>W</sub> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Watterson, G.A. On the number of segregating sites in genetical models without recombination. Theor. Popul. Biol. 7, 256–276 (1975)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2364">53</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). However, purifying selection on CNSs was not generally as strong as in the highly constrained zero-fold degenerate sites. Similar observations were made for deletion polymorphisms at the population level (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Because analyses of MAF spectra only examined segregating variation and ignored the level of polymorphism, these results provide independent validation of the action of purifying selection and limit the possibility of low divergence in CNSs arising from mutation cold spots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Katzman, S. et al. Human genome ultraconserved elements are ultraselected. Science 317, 915 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2375">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Casillas, S., Barbadilla, A. &amp; Bergman, C.M. Purifying selection maintains highly conserved noncoding sequences in Drosophila. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 2222–2234 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2378">55</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Evidence of selection on CNSs in populations."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Evidence of selection on CNSs in populations.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="297"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>MAF distributions are shown at polymorphic sites within a population of 80 <i>A. thaliana</i> individuals (left) and a population of 13 <i>C. grandiflora</i> individuals with 26 haplotypes (right). All types of CNSs show lower population diversity and higher MAF compared to fourfold-degenerate (4D) and intronic sites, confirming that a substantial fraction of CNSs are under selective pressure. Whereas most types of CNSs have similar diversity levels, intronic CNSs seem to be under the weakest selective pressure, and smRNA CNSs seem to be under the strongest selective pressure. 0D, zero-fold degenerate.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Distribution of CNSs in Brassicaceae and other plants</h3><p>The fraction of <i>A. lyrata</i> CNSs for which homologs could be detected in other plant genomes was determined on the basis of sequence similarity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). Whereas most Brassicaceae genomes contained homologs for more than 75% of these CNSs, the early branching <i>A. arabicum</i> genome had homologs for only 38%. The two other Brassicaceae with a reduced number of identifiable homologs were those that had undergone whole-genome triplication events, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>L. alabamica</i>, suggesting increased rates of CNS loss after each triplication. The proportion of <i>A. lyrata</i> CNSs with detectable homologs outside Brassicaceae was relatively low, ranging from 0.8% in the phylogenetically distant <i>Oryza sativa</i> to 3.4% in the more recent neighbor <i>C. papaya</i>. CNSs that seemed to predate Brassicaceae divergence were 75-fold enriched for small noncoding RNAs.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="The majority of A. lyrata CNSs are shared with most other Brassicaceae, but few are conserved outside that clade, with the exception of those corresponding to smRNAs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: The majority of <i>A. lyrata</i> CNSs are shared with most other Brassicaceae, but few are conserved outside that clade, with the exception of those corresponding to smRNAs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="348"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><i>A. lyrata</i> CNSs were first symmetrically extended to at least 120 bp, except when this reached into coding exons. These sequences were then aligned against each genome using BLAST. The number of CNSs with at least one hit with an <i>E</i> value below 0.0001 is shown. Whereas smRNA CNSs constitute only 1.1% of eligible CNSs, they account for more than 18% of CNSs mapped to <i>C. papaya</i>, and this fraction increases to 66% for the most distant species considered, <i>O. sativa</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Loss of genes and CNSs after whole-genome triplications</h3><p>The presence of two whole-genome triplications (Br-α and La-α) offered a further opportunity to study the fate of genes and CNSs after independent genome amplifications in closely related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Feldman, M. &amp; Levy, A.A. Allopolyploidy—a shaping force in the evolution of wheat genomes. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 109, 250–258 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2484">56</a></sup>. Alignment chain data indicated that 38% of <i>A. lyrata</i> genes had a unique ortholog in <i>B. rapa</i>, whereas 25% and 7% had two and three copies, respectively. Despite similar estimates for the timing of Br-α and La-α, <i>B. rapa</i> seems to have lost gene copies faster, having kept two (three) copies of only 18% (3%) of <i>A. lyrata</i> genes. Notably, genes kept in three copies in <i>B. rapa</i> were five times more likely to also be kept in three copies in <i>L. alabamica</i> than would be expected if losses had occurred randomly. As previously observed, those genes were enriched for dosage-sensitive pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Thomas, B.C., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Following tetraploidy in an Arabidopsis ancestor, genes were removed preferentially from one homeolog leaving clusters enriched in dose-sensitive genes. Genome Res. 16, 934–946 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2507">57</a></sup> (for example, the response to environmental cadmium ions, false discovery rate (FDR) = 5 × 10<sup>−9</sup>) or stoichiometry-dependent protein complexes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Luo, F., Liu, J. &amp; Li, J. Discovering conditional co-regulated protein complexes by integrating diverse data sources. BMC Syst. Biol. 4 (suppl. 2), S4 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2513">58</a></sup> (for example, the ribosome, FDR = 2.6 × 10<sup>−6</sup>).</p><p>Similarly, CNSs that were preserved in three copies in <i>B. rapa</i> were, depending on their type, two to four times more likely to be preserved in three copies in <i>L. alabamica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Intronic CNSs showed the highest degree of convergent retention, and 5′ UTR CNSs showed the weakest retention. Notably, 136 of the 428 CNSs kept in 3 copies each in <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>L. alabamica</i> were also kept in 2 copies after the At-α duplication, 89% more than would be expected by chance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Many CNSs are transcriptional regulatory elements</h3><p>To test for a function for intergenic CNSs in transcriptional regulation, the regions bound by 13 transcription factors in <i>A. thaliana</i> chromatin immunoprecipitation chip (ChIP-chip) and sequencing (ChIP-seq) data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Muiño, J.M., Hoogstraat, M., van Ham, R.C. &amp; van Dijk, A.D. PRI-CAT: a web-tool for the analysis, storage and visualization of plant ChIP-seq experiments. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W524–W527 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2548">59</a></sup> were combined, and the extent of their overlap with CNSs was assessed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Seventeen percent of bases in distal CNSs were found to overlap a region bound by at least one transcription factor, representing a 13-fold enrichment. Reciprocally, CNSs covered more than 35% of bases in distal bound regions. Similar overlaps were observed for proximal and downstream CNSs, although at slightly lower levels. However, the situation was quite different for intronic and 3′ UTR CNSs, where only 3–4% of bases overlapped a bound region. Additional evidence points to a role in post-transcriptional regulation for many of these intronic and 3′ UTR CNSs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>DNase I footprinting provides a more global perspective on protein binding to the genome. Overall, 44% of CNSs overlapped a recently published set of DNase I–hypersensitive sites (HSSs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Zhang, W., Zhang, T., Wu, Y. &amp; Jiang, J. Genome-wide identification of regulatory DNA elements and protein-binding footprints using signatures of open chromatin in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 24, 2719–2731 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2564">60</a></sup>, four times more than expected by chance. HSS-overlapping CNSs were mostly found in intergenic regions, with 51% (65%) of distal (proximal) CNSs overlapping an HSS but with only 9% of intronic CNSs and 23% of 3′ UTR CNSs showing overlap. Taken together, these results again suggest that CNSs in intronic regions are generally less likely than intergenic CNSs to mediate protein-DNA interactions. SNP density and MAF spectra clearly point to the portions of HSSs that overlap CNSs as the most strongly constrained subregions, whereas HSSs that had no overlap with CNSs showed reduced evidence of selective pressure (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>).</p><p>We identified a set of 971 5-kb regions that were enriched for intergenic CNSs (coverage above 15%). These CNS-rich regions tended to be located next to genes involved in responses to hormonal stimuli, the regulation of transcription and organ development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 5</a>), matching previous reports in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Inada, D.C. et al. Conserved noncoding sequences in the grasses. Genome Res. 13, 2030–2041 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2577">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Thomas, B.C., Rapaka, L., Lyons, E., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Arabidopsis intragenomic conserved noncoding sequence. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 3348–3353 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2580">22</a></sup> and vertebrates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bejerano, G. et al. Ultraconserved elements in the human genome. Science 304, 1321–1325 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2584">4</a></sup>. Intergenic regions surrounding such genes, as well as their introns, were sometimes covered by up to 25–50% by CNSs. In these cases, it is likely that intronic CNSs have a role in transcriptional rather than post-transcriptional regulation.</p><p>Two CNS-rich regions surround the <i>MIR166A</i> and <i>MIR166B</i> loci, which harbor some of the rare CNSs for which homologs can be found outside Brassicaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). These miRNA genes, which are conserved across eudicot and monocot plants, target the stability of members of the homeobox family of transcription factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kim, J. et al. microRNA-directed cleavage of ATHB15 mRNA regulates vascular development in Arabidopsis inflorescence stems. Plant J. 42, 84–94 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2600">61</a></sup> and are crucial to the establishment of biological axes in stem, root and leaf<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Nogueira, F.T. et al. Regulation of small RNA accumulation in the maize shoot apex. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000320 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2604">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Nogueira, F.T., Madi, S., Chitwood, D.H., Juarez, M.T. &amp; Timmermans, M.C. Two small regulatory RNAs establish opposing fates of a developmental axis. Genes Dev. 21, 750–755 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2607">63</a></sup>. Both <i>MIR166A</i> and <i>MIR166B</i> were surrounded by a cluster of intergenic CNSs, including six with clear homologs beyond the Brassicaceae as well as conservation between the two <i>A. thaliana</i> loci. Other miRNA genes that were associated with clusters of CNSs are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">CNSs are enriched for specific sequence motifs</h3><p>By highlighting genomic regions of likely regulatory function, CNSs facilitated the identification of regulatory motifs for transcription factors or RNA-binding proteins. Each type of CNS was found to be enriched for particular motifs, on the basis of a <i>z</i>-score calculation that compared the number of occurrences of motifs 6–8 nt in length in CNSs to permuted versions of the same motifs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Stark, A. et al. Discovery of functional elements in 12 Drosophila genomes using evolutionary signatures. Nature 450, 219–232 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2635">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Many of the motifs identified were associated with the binding preferences of ubiquitous transcription factors (G-box, E-box, W-box, EIRE, GT-1 and TATA-binding elements) and were enriched in all types of intergenic CNSs, suggesting that these CNSs may have similar regulatory roles. For example, the abscisic acid–linked G-box (CACGTG) and the calcium signaling–sensitive E-box (CATGTG) motifs, when grouped together under the CAYRTGTC motif (with Y representing C or T and R representing A or G), were four- to sevenfold enriched only in intergenic CNSs. The motif enrichment analysis was repeated on subsets of CNSs associated with genes with similar functions, as determined on the basis of GO-Slim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The Gene Ontology Consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2645">64</a></sup>, identifying a large number of known and new putative regulatory motifs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="CNSs are enriched for sequence motifs with evidence of constraint."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: CNSs are enriched for sequence motifs with evidence of constraint.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2684/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2684_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="428"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Motifs found to be enriched (<i>z</i> score &gt; 4) in at least one type of CNS. Numbers and coloring correspond to the fold enrichment compared to the permuted version of the motif (red, enrichment; blue, depletion). Motif annotations come from the PLACE database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Higo, K., Ugawa, Y., Iwamoto, M. &amp; Korenaga, T. Plant cis-acting regulatory DNA elements (PLACE) database: 1999. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 297–300 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2674">74</a></sup> (W, A or T; K, A or C; M, G or T; S, C or G; Y, C or T; N, any base). (<b>b</b>–<b>d</b>) Characteristics of bases in and around proximal upstream instances of the CACGTG motifs located in CNSs. (<b>b</b>) SNP density in the <i>A. thaliana</i> population (blue) and MAF distribution in the <i>C. grandiflora</i> population (red). (<b>c</b>) Position-specific average PhyloP scores over the set of motif instances. (<b>d</b>) Position-specific PhyloP scores for every instance of the set of motifs, ranging from −1 (non-conserved, blue) to +1 (highly conserved, red).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2684/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Likely reflecting the binding contexts relative to the TSS needed for a functional site, diversity estimated in the population of 80 <i>A. thaliana</i> lines was 7-fold lower at G-box sequences in CNSs compared to G-box sequences located outside CNSs. SNP density within G-box sequences in CNSs showed constraint in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. grandiflora</i> populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig6">Fig. 6b</a>) and in interspecies conservation profiles (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Fig6">Fig. 6c,d</a>). Notably, positions immediately flanking most G-box sequences showed a reduced level of conservation compared to overall CNSs, possibly highlighting spatial constraints on the placement of other regulatory elements.</p><p>Several motifs of unknown function were strongly enriched in 5′ UTRs but not elsewhere, with some exhibiting strong strand bias, hinting at a role in post-transcriptional regulation.
