Others were found in both proximal and 5′ UTR CNSs (TATA box and GA track), suggesting a role in transcription initiation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>Although the annotation of protein-coding and some small noncoding RNA genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> has become increasingly complete, until now, no high-resolution map of regulatory regions existed. Here we report the first genome-wide high-resolution atlas of noncoding regions under selection in crucifers. Because the detection of CNSs is based on the comparison of a large number of closely related species, the sensitivity of this map is higher than that in previous studies based on pairs or sets of more distant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hupalo, D. &amp; Kern, A.D. Conservation and functional element discovery in 20 angiosperm plant genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. published online; 10.1093/molbev/mst082 (27 May 2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2741">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Lyons, E. et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 148, 1772–1781 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2744">65</a></sup> or on intragenomic comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Thomas, B.C., Rapaka, L., Lyons, E., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Arabidopsis intragenomic conserved noncoding sequence. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 3348–3353 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2748">22</a></sup>, resulting in an eight to tenfold increase in the number of constrained regions identified.</p><p>Our analysis shows that at least 5% of noncoding sites in <i>A. thaliana</i> seem to have been evolving under some form of purifying selection in Brassicaceae. Our estimate of the proportion of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome that is constrained, combined with experimental estimates of substitution rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2761">66</a></sup> (7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> substitution per site per generation), yields a lower bound on the deleterious substitution rate of at least 0.15 bases per individual per generation, which is comparable to the conservative estimate of 0.1 from a large mutation accumulation experiment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Schultz, S.T., Lynch, M. &amp; Willis, J.H. Spontaneous deleterious mutation in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 11393–11398 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2767">67</a></sup>.</p><p>The number of genomes analyzed in this study and their divergence relative to each other are comparable to those used in similar comparative genomics studies of mammals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bejerano, G. et al. Ultraconserved elements in the human genome. Science 304, 1321–1325 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2774">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Ahituv, N., Prabhakar, S., Poulin, F., Rubin, E.M. &amp; Couronne, O. Mapping cis-regulatory domains in the human genome using multi-species conservation of synteny. Hum. Mol. Genet. 14, 3057–3063 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2777">68</a></sup> and fruit flies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Stark, A. et al. Discovery of functional elements in 12 Drosophila genomes using evolutionary signatures. Nature 450, 219–232 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2781">7</a></sup>. It is consequently possible to contrast the properties of their CNSs. The regulatory complexity of a genome can be approximated by the number of bases in CNSs, normalized by gene number. In <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, this regulatory complexity amounts to 160 bp per gene, slightly more than in yeast (<span class="stix">∼</span>110 bp per gene) but substantially less than in animals (worms, <span class="stix">∼</span>600 bp per gene; fruit flies, <span class="stix">∼</span>2,500 bp per gene; mammals, <span class="stix">∼</span>5,000 bp per gene)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Siepel, A. et al. Evolutionarily conserved elements in vertebrate, insect, worm, and yeast genomes. Genome Res. 15, 1034–1050 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2791">5</a></sup>. This finding suggests that noncoding regulatory mechanisms in plants are intermediate in complexity between those of yeast and worms and is consistent with the hypothesis that plants obtain regulatory complexity via gene or entire-genome duplication rather than from noncoding regulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Lockton, S. &amp; Gaut, B.S. Plant conserved non-coding sequences and paralogue evolution. Trends Genet. 21, 60–65 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2796">69</a></sup>. Alternatively, this low CNS-to-gene ratio might be caused by a high rate of turnover or streamlining of regulatory regions, possibly linked to frequent whole-genome duplications, resulting in many such CNSs going undetected by our approach.</p><p>The most highly conserved noncoding sequences identified were on average <span class="stix">∼</span>70 bp in length with <span class="stix">∼</span>0.15 substitutions per site, which is much lower than the 100% conservation over 200 bp used to define mammalian ultraconserved elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bejerano, G. et al. Ultraconserved elements in the human genome. Science 304, 1321–1325 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2803">4</a></sup>. Nonetheless, the set of 2,012 most highly conserved noncoding sequences (with, at most, 0.5 substitutions per site over at least 50 bp; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 9</a>) stands out from the rest of the CNSs in a manner that is reminiscent of ultraconserved elements, clustering around genes involved in the regulation of embryonic and post-embryonic development. Because the most recent common ancestor of plants and vertebrates was unicellular and, hence, likely lacked developmental patterning, this finding suggests that similar patterns of CNS control may have evolved independently in the two kingdoms—a noteworthy example of convergent evolution of genomic organization.</p><p>Crucifer CNSs differ from animal CNSs in the way that they are associated with their putative target genes: distal CNSs are comparatively less frequent in Brassicaceae than in animals, and first introns are generally depleted of CNSs, unlike in mammals where CNSs distribute roughly symmetrically around TSSs, and first introns are enriched for regulatory elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Margulies, E.H., Blanchette, M., Haussler, D. &amp; Green, E.D. Identification and characterization of multi-species conserved sequences. Genome Res. 13, 2507–2518 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2814">70</a></sup>. These differences in CNS distribution may reflect some of the differences in intron-exon structures between plants and animals. In vertebrates, the first intron may be relatively extended, whereas, in plants, it tends to be shorter with alternative splicing more frequently driving intron retention<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Hong, X., Scofield, D.G. &amp; Lynch, M. Intron size, abundance, and distribution within untranslated regions of genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 2392–2404 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2818">71</a></sup>, thereby potentially limiting the first intron as a site for regulatory CNSs.</p><p>Together with findings from other ongoing investigations, the resources introduced here will help to establish the properties of the constrained portion of the noncoding genome of the crucifers, in much the same way as similar projects have in other eukaryotic species. Combined with the application of systematic genome-wide experimental assays<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="ENCODE Project Consortium. An integrated encyclopedia of DNA elements in the human genome. Nature 489, 57–74 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2825">72</a></sup>, this atlas of noncoding selection in the Brassicaceae will further open the door to the detailed characterization of the <i>cis</i> regulome of these species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Sequencing and assembly.</h3><p>The genomes of <i>L. alabamica</i>, <i>A. arabicum</i> and <i>S. irio</i> were assembled from Illumina paired-end reads (2 × 105 nt with a nominal 64-nt gap on the Genome Analyzer IIx platform; 55–110× coverage) and mate-pair reads (2 × 105 nt with 5-kb and 10-kb insert sizes on the Genome Analyzer IIx and HiSeq 2000 platforms). Libraries and reads were generated in accordance with Illumina protocols, with special attention paid to gentle shearing of mate-pair circular DNA. Reads were trimmed for quality (3′ trimming starting at the first position with <i>Q</i> &lt; 32) and assembled with the Ray assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Boisvert, S., Laviolette, F. &amp; Corbeil, J. Ray: simultaneous assembly of reads from a mix of high-throughput sequencing technologies. J. Comput. Biol. 17, 1519–1533 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2856">78</a></sup> using a <i>K</i>-mer size of 31 to 41 (optimized per genome). Mate-pair reads were filtered for duplicates using a Bloom filter (pybloomfaster; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec22">URLs</a>) and for potential false mates on the edges of contigs, resulting in approximately 12× (5×) coverage for 5-kb (10-kb) inserts for each species and were then scaffolded using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2867">79</a></sup> with a <i>K</i>-mer size of 61 and with gap filling enabled.</p><p>The genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> (ssp. Chiifu-401-42; pre-publication data from Wang <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2882">27</a></sup>), <i>S. parvula</i> (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA63667">PRJNA63667</a>), <i>A. lyrata</i> (ssp. <i>lyrata</i>; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA41137">PRJNA41137</a>), <i>A. thaliana</i> (Col-0; TAIR9/TAIR10, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA10719">PRJNA10719</a>), <i>E. salsugineum</i> (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA80723">PRJNA80723</a>) and <i>C. rubella</i> (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA13878">PRJNA13878</a>) were obtained either directly from their assemblers or from data published at the time of the release of the genome.</p><p>Genome completeness was assessed relative to the total and expected assembly length, the count of <i>A. thaliana</i> orthologs and the percentage of complete, highly conserved eukaryotic genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The <i>A. arabicum</i> genome was further validated against a set of physical mapping data (Keygene) that showed near-perfect concordance between assembled scaffolds and BAC contigs. The <i>S. irio</i> genome was compared against BAC and BAC-end data in GenBank. Although sequences were mostly concordant, the BAC data was from a tetraploid species and was consequently not expected to be completely similar. The <i>L. alabamica</i> genome was examined in several TE-rich extended loci (for example, the <i>S</i> locus) that had been assembled using long-read fosmid sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Chantha, S.C., Herman, A.C., Platts, A.E., Vekemans, X. &amp; Schoen, D.J. Secondary evolution of a self-incompatibility locus in the brassicaceae genus leavenworthia. PLoS Biol. 11, e1001560 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2963">80</a></sup> and showed near-perfect concordance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">CCP analysis in <i>L. alabamica.</i></h3><p>Preparation of chromosome spreads from young anthers and BAC painting probes, as well as multicolor FISH, followed the protocols described by Mandáková <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mandáková, T. Analysis of plant meiotic chromosomes by chromosome painting. Methods Mol. Biol. 990, 13–24 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2981">81</a></sup>. In total, 237 chromosome-specific BACs from <i>A. thaliana</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>23 Mb) were used as painting probes. The following <i>A. thaliana</i> BAC contigs were applied to identify 8 ancestral genomic blocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schranz, M.E., Lysak, M.A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABC's of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535–542 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22561e2991">23</a></sup> on <i>L. alabamica</i> chromosomes: block A (31 BACs: T25K16–T29M8; 6.7 Mb), block O (24 BACs: F6N15–T1J1; 2.5 Mb), block P (13 BACs: T3H13–T22B4; 1.3 Mb), block Q (32 BACs: T20O7–T8M17; 2.6 Mb), block R (33 BACs: F7J8–T6G21; 7.4 Mb), block V (22 BACs: MBD2–K23F3; 2.4 Mb), block W (56 BACs: K21P3–MMN10; 4.3 Mb) and block X (26 BACs: MUP24–K9I9; 2.5 Mb).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome annotation.</h3><p>All nine genomes were annotated for genic regions using Maker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Cantarel, B.L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3007">82</a></sup> in conjunction with FGENESH and FGENESH+ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3010">83</a>), Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3014">84</a></sup>, SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3018">85</a></sup> and BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3022">86</a></sup> for transcript mapping. Repetitive regions and TEs were annotated with RepeatMasker using repeat models determined on a per-species basis obtained from RepeatModeler (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#MOESM21">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>In addition to annotation of the <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes, we combined sequenced mRNA from whole <i>A. lyrata</i> plants (Illumina, strand-specific RNA sequencing, 2 × 80 nt; R. Clark, personal communication; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA207497">PRJNA207497</a>), archived mRNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Gan, X. et al. Multiple reference genomes and transcriptomes for Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 477, 419–423 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3049">87</a></sup> (NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR019209">SRR019209</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR019183">SRR019183</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR064165">SRR064165</a>) and small RNA sequence data from both SOLiD and Illumina platforms (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR040402">SRR040402</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR072809">SRR072809</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR034856">SRR034856</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR051926">SRR051926</a>). Reads were aligned both with Novoalign (Novocraft) with high-alignment stringency and SpliceMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Au, K.F., Jiang, H., Lin, L., Xing, Y. &amp; Wong, W.H. Detection of splice junctions from paired-end RNA-seq data by SpliceMap. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, 4570–4578 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3104">88</a></sup> for exon-spanning reads. Expression tracks were then lifted over between the two reference genomes to aid annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Whole-genome alignments.</h3><p>Each genome was soft masked and aligned to <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>E. salsugineum</i> (primary and secondary reference genomes, respectively) using lastz<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Harris, R.S. Improved Pairwise Alignment of Genomic DNA. PhD thesis, Penn. State Univ. (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3122">89</a></sup>, and chaining<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Kent, W.J., Baertsch, R., Hinrichs, A., Miller, W. &amp; Haussler, D. Evolution's cauldron: duplication, deletion, and rearrangement in the mouse and human genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 100, 11484–11489 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3126">90</a></sup> and assembling collinear alignment blocks separated by gaps of &lt;100 kb were then performed. We filtered for orthologous chains by retaining chains in decreasing order of score that did not substantially overlap previously selected chains in the non-reference genome (chains could overlap in the reference genome). This filter effectively separates orthologs from α paralogs, while allowing more recent whole-genome duplications to be properly represented. In the case of the two genomes with whole-genome triplications (<i>B. rapa</i> and <i>L. alabamica</i>), genomic regions were subdivided into three groups of chains, where each group contained non-overlapping chaining. We obtained a 13-way multiple alignment using the Multiz<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Blanchette, M. et al. Aligning multiple genomic sequences with the threaded blockset aligner. Genome Res. 14, 708–715 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3137">91</a></sup> progressive alignment program, following phylogenetic order, using <i>A. lyrata</i> as the reference for lineage 1 and <i>E. salsugineum</i> as the reference for lineage 2. For the purpose of measuring sequence conservation of a region, the most conserved of the paralogs in <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>L. alabamica</i> were retained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Determination of the fraction of sites under selection.</h3><p>Our approach to estimate the fraction of sites under selection is based on that of Watterson <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Waterston, R.H. et al. Initial sequencing and comparative analysis of the mouse genome. Nature 420, 520–562 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3165">92</a></sup>, adapted to use site-specific conservation scores. PhyloP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pollard, K.S., Hubisz, M.J., Rosenbloom, K.R. &amp; Siepel, A. Detection of nonneutral substitution rates on mammalian phylogenies. Genome Res. 20, 110–121 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3169">45</a></sup> was used to measure position-specific conservation levels on the basis of nine-way alignment, using a model of freely evolving sites obtained from fourfold-degenerate sites of the same alignment and on the JGI gene annotation of <i>A. lyrata</i>. The fraction of sites under selection in a given set of sites R was obtained as follows. Let S be a subset of the nine species considered and let R<sub>S</sub> be a subset of sites from R that has nucleotides in S and gaps in the other species. PhyloP scores were discretized in 1,000 bins. For each S, let <i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>) be the distribution of discretized PhyloP scores obtained from fourfold-degenerate sites by replacing nucleotides in species outside S by gaps and calculating the distribution of PhyloP scores. Let <i>f</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) be the observed distribution of PhyloP scores in R<sub>S</sub>. We express <i>f</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) as a mixture of <i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>) and <i>f</i><sub>FS</sub>(<i>x</i>), the unknown distribution of scores for sites under selection in R<sub>S</sub>. Specifically, we estimated <i>α</i><sub>RS</sub> so that <i>f</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) <i>= α</i><sub>RS</sub><i>f</i><sub>FS</sub>(<i>x</i>) + (1 −<i>α</i><sub>RS</sub>) <i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>). Let <i>F</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>) and <i>F</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) be the cumulative distributions of <i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>) and <i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>), respectively. Let <i>x</i>* be the value for which <i>F</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>)/<i>F</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) is maximized (excluding values of <i>x</i> for which either of the two cumulative distributions has a value less than 0.1), and let <i>r</i><sub>max</sub><i>= F</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>*)/<i>F</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>*). We obtain <i>α</i><sub>RS</sub> = <i>Σ</i><sub><i>x = x</i>*...1,000</sub><i>f</i><sub>RS</sub>(<i>x</i>) − (<i>f</i><sub>NS</sub>(<i>x</i>)/<i>r</i><sub>max</sub>). Finally, the fraction under selection for region <i>R</i> is determined as <i>Σ</i><sub>S</sub><i>α</i><sub>RS</sub> |R<sub>S</sub>|/|R|. Note that, because not all fourfold-degenerate sites are truly unconstrained, our estimate of the fraction under selection in R is a lower bound.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">CNS identification.</h3><p>CNSs were identified as regions located beyond annotated coding sequences in the <i>A. lyrata</i> reference genome that showed high PhastCons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Siepel, A. et al. Evolutionarily conserved elements in vertebrate, insect, worm, and yeast genomes. Genome Res. 15, 1034–1050 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3376">5</a></sup> score (&gt;0.82) over an extended length (&gt;7 nt) and did not include a region of more than 12 nt with low PhastCons score (&lt;0.55). To facilitate the comparison of incompletely sequenced genomes, insertions, deletions and missing orthologous sequences were not penalized, and CNSs were not required to be present in all nine species. The parameters were refined relative to an 800,000-base sequence generated from concatenated fourfold-degenerate sites within which a CNS FDR of &lt;1% was required. Independent verifications based on evolutionary signatures of coding sequences using RNAcode<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Washietl, S. et al. RNAcode: robust discrimination of coding and noncoding regions in comparative sequence data. RNA 17, 578–594 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3380">93</a></sup>, the absence of splice sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Au, K.F., Jiang, H., Lin, L., Xing, Y. &amp; Wong, W.H. Detection of splice junctions from paired-end RNA-seq data by SpliceMap. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, 4570–4578 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3384">88</a></sup> and a uniform density of stop codons suggested that very few CNSs correspond with unannotated protein-coding exons.</p><p>Candidate CNSs were assigned a location category, and only those in the small noncoding and UTR classes were allowed to overlap expressed regions. CNSs were rejected that formed extensions of coding sequences (due to the PhastCons smoothing algorithm) and that overlapped other evidence of a potential coding role. Because UTR annotation, particularly the transition between coding sequences and UTRs, is not error free, we expect a slightly higher false positive rate for the UTR CNSs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Motif enrichment.</h3><p>The significance of the enrichment of motifs of 5 to 11 nt in length in CNSs was determined by a <i>z</i> score representing a comparison of the frequency of a motif's occurrence in all CNSs to the distribution of the occurrence of all permutations of the motif sequence in CNSs. This approach was selected to account for substantial base bias at single- and multi-base levels between CNSs and surrounding promoter regions. Enriched motifs were then clustered with those with a minimal edit distance and combined into IUPAC and PWM representations. Motif characterization was determined using the PLACE, TAIR and JASPAR databases. Enrichment of motifs in upstream, intronic, UTR and downstream CNSs relative to ontology groups was determined relative to the GO-Slim ontology annotation of the immediately proximate gene.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Population genomics analyses.</h3><p>Alignments for the genomes of 80 Eurasian <i>A. thaliana</i> plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Cao, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of multiple Arabidopsis thaliana populations. Nat. Genet. 43, 956–963 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3413">51</a></sup> against the TAIR9/TAIR10 reference were obtained from the 1001 Genomes Project (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2684#Sec22">URLs</a>). For measures of diversity over specific regions, those locations with base calls in all 80 samples were used, whereas, for more general comparisons with comparative genomics data, calls in at least 40 samples were required. Diversity estimates from the genomes of a population of 13 Greek <i>C. grandiflora</i> plants (26 haplotypes) were generated by aligning Illumina paired-end data to the genome of its close relative <i>C. rubella</i> using the STAMPY-GATK pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Lunter, G. &amp; Goodson, M. Stampy: a statistical algorithm for sensitive and fast mapping of Illumina sequence reads. Genome Res. 21, 936–939 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3427">94</a></sup>. Again, 26 base calls were required for diversity estimates, and regions with extremes of sequence depth or low quality were excluded. Pipelines for population genomics analyses were developed using Perl and Python languages and Bio++ libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Dutheil, J. et al. Bio++: a set of C++ libraries for sequence analysis, phylogenetics, molecular evolution and population genetics. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 188 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2684#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d22561e3431">95</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">URLs.</h3><p>RepeatMasker, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>; pybloomfaster, <a href="https://github.com/brentp/pybloomfaster">https://github.com/brentp/pybloomfaster</a>; 1001 Genomes Project, <a href="http://1001genomes.org/data/MPI/MPICao2010/releases/">http://1001genomes.org/data/MPI/MPICao2010/releases/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Accession codes.</h3><p><i>A. arabicum</i> genome, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA202984">PRJNA202984</a>; <i>S. irio</i> genome, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA202979">PRJNA202979</a>; <i>L. alabamica</i> genome, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA202983">PRJNA202983</a>. All sequences, genome annotations, pairwise and multiple alignments, conservation scores and CNSs are available for visualization and download on a local installation of the UCSC Genome Browser at <a href="http://mustang.biol.mcgill.ca:8885">http://mustang.biol.mcgill.ca:8885</a>.</p></div></div></section>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Mathieu+Blanchette" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Mathieu+Blanchette%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>The study was conceived by M.B., S.I.W., A.M.M., T.E.B., D.J.S., P.M.H. and J.R.S. Computational experiments were designed by A.H., A.E.P., A.M.M., S.I.W., T.E.B. and M.B. E.F., Z.J.-L., J.C.P., M.E.S., D.J.S. and T.E.B. obtained material for genome sequencing for <i>L. alabamica</i>, <i>S. irio</i> and <i>A. arabicum</i>. A.E.P., K.D. and T.E.B. sequenced the DNA, and A.E.P. assembled the genomes, using additional data provided by C.D.T., P.P.E., M.E.S., E.v.d.B. and J.C.P. Additional RNA sequencing data were obtained from J.G.S., <i>B. rapa</i> genome sequence data were provided by X.W., and <i>E. salsugineum</i> genome data were provided by J.S., D.E.J. and K.S.S. T.M. and M.A.L. performed the multicolor FISH study on <i>L. alabamica</i>. P.M.H. and A.E.P. performed the gene annotation, D.R.H. and T.E.B. annotated TEs, and M.L. identified structural RNAs. Multiple-genome alignments and identification and analysis of CNSs were performed by A.E.P., A.H., E.V. and M.B. Population genetics analyses were performed by A.H., A.E.P., R.J.W., K.M.H., A.M.M., A.E.P. and S.I.W. The manuscript was written primarily by A.H., A.E.P., S.I.W. and M.B., with input from all coauthors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2684/email/correspondent/c1/new">Alan M Moses</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2684/email/correspondent/c2/new">Thomas E Bureau</a> or <a id="corresp-c3" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2684/email/correspondent/c3/new">Stephen I Wright</a> or <a id="corresp-c4" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2684/email/correspondent/c4/new">Mathieu Blanchette</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>
